Role & context
You are an education strategy analyst helping me explore the feasibility of a future La Scuola International School High School (grades 9–12) in San Francisco. La Scuola is an IB, Reggio-inspired, international community with a strong identity in language learning (Italian heritage, global-mindedness). I’m exploring a model where the main curriculum is in English, with robust options for a second and third language, and Italian offered as an option (not necessarily the primary language of instruction).

Research goal
Produce a market and program feasibility study for an English-dominant IB high school with strong multilingual pathways, designed to compete in the Bay Area independent school market.

Competitors to include (compare directly)

International High School of San Francisco (or International School of San Francisco high school, if applicable)

Lick-Wilmerding High School (Lick)

St. Ignatius College Preparatory (SI)

International School of Silicon Valley (and note if it is now part of another institution / renamed)

“Bilingual Diploma IB” pathway schools (IB Bilingual Diploma approach)

Deliverables (format exactly like this)

Executive summary (1 page)

The 3 most viable HS models for La Scuola

The 3 biggest risks

The 3 strongest differentiators we can own

A preliminary recommendation: Explore / Pilot / Pause with rationale

Competitor comparison table (one row per school)
Include columns:

School type (IB/AP/independent/Catholic/etc.)

Tuition range (if available)

Admissions selectivity (qualitative)

Program signature (what they are known for)

Language offerings (2nd/3rd language depth)

IB status (DP/CP/AP instead)

Student outcomes signals (college counseling reputation, placement patterns, etc.—use only defensible public info)

Strengths vs La Scuola opportunity gaps

Notes + sources to verify (list what to look up)

IB program model options for La Scuola (3–4 models)
For each model include:

Schedule and pathway logic (English core + language tracks)

How IB DP would be structured (HL/SL considerations)

How students could pursue IB Bilingual Diploma (what conditions must be met)

Staffing implications (language faculty, TOK/EE, IB coordination)

Student profile it serves best

What makes it distinctive in SF market

Language pathways design
Propose 3 concrete language pathways:

“Strong second language” track

“Second + third language” track

“Italian focus” track (Italian as option, strong but not immersion)
Include: entry points (9th grade vs later), placement approach, support for heritage speakers vs beginners, and outcomes.

Market positioning & messaging
Draft:

1 positioning statement

3 key messages for parents

3 key messages for students

3 proof points we must build (facilities, IB results plan, language outcomes, internships, etc.)

What data we must collect next (action plan)
List the next 10–15 data points to gather (with priority order), plus where to find them (school websites, CAIS, IB directory, Niche/GreatSchools cautiously, tours, parent interviews).

Constraints & quality rules

Be clear where you’re certain vs hypothesizing.

Do not invent facts (tuition, programs, IB status, enrollment). If unknown, mark “Needs verification”.

Focus on SF/Bay Area parent decision drivers: rigor, outcomes, student support, identity, languages, wellbeing, college counseling, facilities, commute, culture.

Keep tone professional, strategic, and actionable.

Output style
Use headings, bullets, and one clean table. Keep it concise but decision-ready.

Optional add-on prompt (if you want it to feel more “La Scuola”)

Paste this after the main prompt:

“Make sure the recommendations align with a school culture that values inquiry, international-mindedness, wellbeing, and language learning, and propose at least two signature programs La Scuola could own (e.g., global citizenship capstone, multilingual debate/diplomacy track, arts/design + IB, internships/community partnerships).”

If you tell me (1) the target opening year you have in mind and (2) whether you’re imagining DP only or DP + another pathway (AP/CP), I can tighten the prompt even more—but the one above will already give you a strong first output.

Post

Introducing Gamma 3.0: a generational leap in how people share ideas.

I feel an immense sense of pride today — but also gratitude.

The truth is, launches like this don’t come easy. Behind every shiny announcement are late nights, tough choices, and moments of real doubt.

Through it all, the Gamma team has been nothing short of extraordinary. I’ve seen engineers pull off miracles in the past few days, designers sweat every detail until they felt right, and teammates step up for each other.

For nearly five years, this team has been chasing our mission: to help anyone bring their ideas to life.
 
And today, with Gamma 3.0, this mission feels closer than ever to reality.

Two breakthroughs I’m excited to share:

1. Gamma Agent

AI that works like a true collaborator. With one prompt, you can make sweeping edits across a deck:

- Ask Agent to “make it more visual,” and it will scan your slides for opportunities to better showcase your data.
- Upload rough notes or a screenshot, and Agent will synthesize the info, search the web for gaps, and match your brand.

2. Gamma API

Bring AI-powered storytelling into your workflows. 

- Imagine: after a client meeting, your notes automatically become a tailored, on-brand recap deck that lands in their inbox—before you even leave the room.
- Or your course outline gets translated into 60 languages without pressing a button. 

The possibilities are endless now that you can connect Gamma to all your favorite tools.

Bonus: 
We’re also launching our Team & Business plans, bringing visual storytelling to organizations of all sizes, and a new Ultra plan for our power users looking to push the boundaries even further.

We can’t wait to see what you bring to life with 3.0.


4,714 · 658 comments

Post

The New York Times profiled a start-up with 28 employees serving nearly 50 million users.

That company is us.

The traditional startup playbook: raise massive funding, hire hundreds of employees, and worry about profitability "later."

But there's another way.

Everyone at Gamma could fit in a small restaurant.

We're not just surviving—we've been profitable for 15+ consecutive months, with revenue growing month over month, and lifetime negative net burn (we have more money in the bank than we've raised).

This isn't an accident. We've deliberately designed our organization to maximize impact per person.

Instead of creating specialist silos, we hire versatile generalists who can solve problems across domains. Rather than building management hierarchies, we find player-coaches who both lead and execute.

Our team leverages AI tools throughout our workflow - Claude for data analysis, Cursor for coding efficiency, NotebookLM for customer research synthesis. These aren't just productivity hacks; they're force multipliers. 

Examples:

— When our growth PM needed better analytics, he didn't file a ticket with a data team—he built a self-serve system that anyone can use without SQL knowledge.

— When our marketing lead needed to understand our customers better, she fed thousands of interactions into an LLM and created actionable personas that now guide our entire strategy.

— When our design team needs to test a hypothesis, we create a rapid prototype and show it to our power users.

What we're seeing isn't just about "doing more with less." It's about fundamentally changing what's possible per person.

The most valuable employees aren't specialists who excel in narrow domains - they're resourceful problem-solvers who continuously expand their capabilities.

This approach creates remarkable resilience. Since everyone understands multiple functions, we don't have single points of failure when someone leaves or moves to another project.

If you're building today, the question isn't how quickly you can scale headcount … it's how much impact you can create with the smallest possible team.

The future belongs to tiny teams of extraordinary people.


6,871 · 742 comments

Post

"I'm too old to start a company."

If Morris Chang had believed that at 55, we wouldn't have TSMC - the $700B company whose chips are the invisible foundation of our entire digital world.

He'd already been passed over for promotion. Failed at another company. In Silicon Valley terms, his career was supposed to be over.

Instead, he moved to Taiwan and created not just a company but an entirely new business model that revolutionized how technology is built.

The youth-obsessed tech narrative tells us innovation belongs to 20-somethings in hoodies: Gates (19). Jobs (21). Zuckerberg (19).

In reality, the data shows a different story:

- The average successful founder is 42
- A 50-year-old is nearly twice as likely to build a high-growth company as a 30-year-old
- The least successful founders? Those we glorify most? Early 20-somethings

Chang's genius wasn't technical brilliance, either.

It was seeing what everyone else missed after 30 years in semiconductors—that companies designing chips and companies manufacturing them could be separate entities.

"I could not have done it sooner," Chang said. "I don't think anybody could have done it sooner. Because I was the first one."

This insight (which transformed the entire industry) wasn't born from “youthful ambition,” but from decades of understanding problems deeply enough to see their solutions.

Because experience isn't baggage:

It's context.
It's pattern recognition.
It's knowing which risks are actually worth taking.

Today, every iPhone, every AI server, every Tesla runs on chips manufactured by the company Chang built after most would have settled into retirement.

So the next time you think your moment has passed - remember that your greatest contribution might still be ahead, built on the foundation of everything you've learned so far.

What could you build with all your hard-earned wisdom?

When we started Gamma, the bet was simple: people have way more ideas than they have time to make them presentable.

Notes, docs, meeting recaps, email threads, the thinking is all there. But the jump from "rough thinking" to "something I'd actually share with a stakeholder" is still way too painful.

Superhuman Go lives right where you work in your browser, across your tabs. With Gamma inside it, you can take whatever you're looking at and turn it into a visual story without any context switching.

4d • 


Most founders don't start with a calling. They start with a problem that irritates them enough to keep poking at it.

The "calling" shows up later - after users pour in, teammates believe in the mission, the product gets sharper. What felt like a side project becomes the thing you'd fight to protect.

Scott Adams failed at more careers than most people attempt. His conclusion:

"Success caused passion more than passion caused success."

The best founders I know picked a problem they could tolerate and let building teach them what to care about. The caring grew out of the work.

Conviction begins as a hypothesis and becomes belief only through contact with reality. The only way in is through building.

At Gamma, signups flatlined after our first launch. The "calling" came after we decided to keep at it and figure out retention. Once people started sharing it on their own, the passion created itself.

Find something you're willing to build long enough for passion to find you.

What problem keeps pulling you back even when you try to move on?… more

5d • 


You spent months building it. Your user will spend 3 seconds deciding if they care.

They're at work, running late, between meetings, worried about their kid. They will bounce in a fraction of a second.

Stewart Butterfield, founder of Slack, learned this from restaurant websites.

Think about any cluttered restaurant site: ambient music auto-playing, slideshow of the dining room. Meanwhile, you're just hunting for the address.

Butterfield calls this "the owner's delusion." Owners believe their thing is so important that people will tolerate friction.

Building something changes how you see it. You know every feature, every edge case, every workaround.

You stop being a user. You become the author of a story you expect everyone else to already know.

When you've written every chapter, it's nearly impossible to see what's confusing for someone reading page one.

Visitors don't arrive empty-handed. They clicked an ad. Searched a term. Heard a recommendation. They have intent - the question is whether you're speaking to it or burying it.

Here's a test: Ask your last five signups what they came looking for. Then open your landing page. How far do they have to scroll to find it?

They don't see what you built. They see where they got stuck.

With AI making it easier than ever to ship, this trap is only growing. More builders, more features, more friction disguised as functionality.

What are you making users hunt for?… more

8 years ago, I spent over $200k to exercise my startup options.

I didn't have enough saved, so I took out a big loan.

A few years later the company was acquired. Decent outcome. But after paying back the loan and taxes, my startup equity actually cost me money.

The Wall Street Journal shared my story last week. That experience shaped how we built equity at Gamma.

When we started the company, we made a vow: if Gamma succeeded, our team should share in that success. Without having to pay upfront.

So we designed our equity program around it, including secondary opportunities with cashless exercise.

As part of our Series B, we completed our first employee tender. $44M led by IVP.

Our early team, anyone with vested options, was allowed to participate. Some have been with us for over five years. None of them had to take out a loan to share in what they helped build.

P.S. Full WSJ piece in the first comment.… more

Ramp's profit is growing 153% year over year. At $1 billion in revenue.

The median public SaaS company grows at 16%. The top 10 grow at 30%.

Most companies get big and get slow. Ramp got big and got faster.

In a letter to customers, CEO Eric Glyman broke down what their AI agents did in a single month. 26 million decisions across $10B in spend.

Two examples that stuck with me:

1. Their travel agent saved an employee named Zain exactly $113.34 on his trip to New York.
2. Their policy agent blocked $291M in out-of-policy transactions.

From $113 to $291 million. Same attention to detail.

The AI cares about $113 because the org is built to care about $113. That's the whole secret.

Conway's Law: your product mirrors the organization that built it. 

Before Ramp even existed, the Capital One CEO mentioned his grandson's hockey gloves arrived late from Amazon.

Within a week, Eric had built a feature securing automatic refunds for late shipments.

Ramp built a 1,200-person org that sweats the small stuff at scale. That's why the AI works at this level of granularity. Glyman calls their customers "heavyweights that move like lightweights."

What your company cares about at 10 people is what your product will care about at 1,000.… more

1w • 


"If this doesn't make me rich, what's the point?"

That sentence is a confession. When founders pitch Tim Draper on money, exits, or competitor size, he sees a red flag.

"A startup is a mission. A great entrepreneur is a missionary. Not a mercenary."

Mercenary pitches have tells:

- They're full of finance nouns: valuation, exit, multiple, comps.

- They treat customers as a route, not a relationship.

- They describe the business like a trade.

Missionary founders have different tells:

- They light up describing the problem, not the market size.

- They talk about customer wins like personal victories.

- They celebrate what their team built and how to support their growth.

The best missions sound irrational at first. Not "here's my wedge into a $10B market." Just "this is broken and it shouldn't be."

Once you've seen how broken the status quo is, you can't look away.

The test for founders:

Would you still work on this if capital markets closed tomorrow?… more

1w • 


"If you only had a year to live, would you take this job?"

Brian Chesky, founder of Airbnb, used to ask this in every interview.

While most consider this a bit extreme, it's the kind of question that forces you to think. Most hiring filters for competence. Brian was filtering for something else: does your work light you up.

Some people want to join because they want what the company produces: cash, status, safety. The job is a toll booth.

Other people work because the work itself is the point. One person says "I took this job because I could." Another says "because I couldn't not."

You can spot which kind of company you're in the moment you walk into it.

Brie Wolfson recently joined Cursor as their Head of Employee Experience. She heard zero chatter about getting rich, even as the valuation skyrocketed.

Being rich is not the same as being done.

A team that doesn't talk about getting rich usually already has what it wants: the work.

The "if you won the lottery" test doesn't reveal whether you care about money or not.

It reveals what your work means to you.… more

2w • 


At 50, he felt like "he had been put out to pasture."

He decided to prove the world wrong and created one of the most valuable companies of all time.

Morris Chang's story is one of the cleanest counterexamples I know to the myth that great success has to come early.

The arc: Failed his PhD qualifying exam. Spent 25 years at Texas Instruments, rising into major management roles. Then got transferred to a staff job.

His words: "put out to the pasture."

He agonized over leaving for more than two years. Finally left. Took another operating role, didn't fit the culture, left again. Two departures in quick succession.

Then Taiwan called.

K.T. Li (Taiwan's economic minister) wanted him to present a business plan for a semiconductor company.

So, at 56, he founded TSMC with a completely new model: manufacture chips for other companies, without competing with them.

The industry reaction: "What the hell is Morris Chang doing?"

The core objection was structural: "Where's the market?"

The fabless industry barely existed.

But Chang had watched this problem for decades: great designers were trapped inside big companies.

They had ideas for chips they wanted to build, but manufacturing plants cost billions. You couldn't be a startup without a factory you couldn't afford.

Rather than betting the market was big, he was betting it would *become* big once that constraint was removed.

TSMC became the infrastructure that let an entire generation of fabless companies exist. Nvidia doesn't own a fab. Neither does AMD, Qualcomm, or Apple's chip division.

Chang wasn’t a college dropout with a world-changing idea. He had 25 years of watching what stopped companies from existing.

If you're over 40, "why you" is rarely the question. You've already earned that.

The real question: what barrier have you spent decades watching that everyone else has learned to live with?… more

2w •

How to AI · Full-time

London Area, United Kingdom · Remote

I build the internet’s best free guides, blogs, and learning paths. My core belief is simple. Everything you need to learn is already online. The real problem is noise, not access.

Most people don’t need a degree or a mentor. They need discipline and the right sources in the right order. I can’t teach discipline yet. But I can remove the overwhelm. I can filter the chaos into clear, practical routes anyone can follow.

My work is to curate, test, and organize the smartest free content on the web so people can actually use it. No gatekeeping. No jargon. No filler. Just focused education for people who want to get better without paying for another useless course.

About
Building new worlds where technology augments human creativity. 
Portfolio at matthieulorrain.com

Creative Technology leader | Business Insider’s 2024 AI Power List | Adweek AI Trailblazer 2025

For the past two decades, I’ve been at the frontier of digital storytelling, leading global teams and shaping creative platforms with some of the world’s most iconic brands and artists, including Donald Glover, Gorillaz, Darren Aronofsky, Star Wars, the NBA, Louis Vuitton, Space Invaders, Lego, and Nike.

My focus is 0→1 innovation, working across product, design, and emerging media to invent the next-generation of content experiences. Whether building experimental formats or designing creative tools, I operate at the intersection of technology, art, and culture.

My mission is to advance human creativity with sustainable technology, championing ideas that enrich our collective future. I try to deliver on that vision by building across disciplines (creative, strategy, design, tech) and growing teams that turn visionary ideas into reality.… more

Post

🎬 Darren Aronofsky just produced his first AI film.
Today, I’m thrilled to share our latest collaboration: ANCESTRA.

👩‍🎨 Directed by the visionary Eliza McNitt, ANCESTRA is a personal story of birth, memory, and transformation, reimagined through the lens of generative AI.

This film is the result of a unique collaboration between Google DeepMind, Primordial Soup (Aronofsky’s new studio), and Google Creative Lab.

🖼️ It blends live-action performance with AI-generated sequences using our next-gen models: Veo, Gemini, and Imagen.

💡What makes this film special:
- Based on Eliza’s own near-death birth experience
- Shot in live action, then extended and reimagined with generative tools
- A poetic fusion of reality and imagination, powered by new technology, guided by deeply human emotion

🎞️ Watch the full film below

📖 And go behind the scenes in this official DeepMind blog: https://lnkd.in/eJs2dffk

This is what happens when filmmakers lead the way, using AI to expand cinema, not replace it. 

🙏 A huge thank you to the many many people who made this project possible.

Storytelling is liquid 💧

Google DeepMind introduces Project Genie 

Project Genie is an experimental research prototype that lets you create and explore virtual worlds. 🌎

Powered by our Genie 3 model, it lets you generate infinite interactive environments.

Here’s how it works:
🔵 Create: Design characters and worlds using text and visual prompts. 
🔵 Refine: Nano Banana Pro makes an image preview that you can adjust.
🔵 Step inside: Your environment is generated in real-time as you move around. 
🔵 Remix: Build on existing worlds or discover new ones in the gallery.

Google rolling this out now to Google AI Ultra subscribers in the U.S. (18+)
The future is liquid.... 💧

✨ This image knows the subway is delayed.

For this test, I asked Google DeepMind Nano Pro to create a 3D isometric poster of an NYC subway line - but first, it had to check the live MTA status via search.

Here is what it produced:
🔴 Line 2 - model detected delays in real time and wrote [Delays] 
🟢 Line 4 - same prompt pattern, live data said [Good Service],

Same layout, same style.
Different visuals, because the world outside the prompt was different.

That is Liquid Content: images that react to time, data, and context instead of staying frozen.

Why this matters:
📊 Dynamic dashboards that look like tiny worlds, updating with markets, weather, or traffic.
 📣 Campaigns that shift tone and visuals based on live scores or local events.
 🧠 Grounded creativity that ties pixels to verifiable signals, not guesswork.

If you could plug an AI image model into any live data source, what would you build first? 👇

💧 Content is liquid.

About
I'm Anton and I care about having positive impact for the world.

I started Lovable because I believe that democratising the ability to create software is one of the highest levers to unlock more creativity and human potential.

Previously I worked with particle physics at CERN, before joining as the first employee and engineer at Sana Labs, where I helped it succeed in improving the lives of millions of learners and raising $80m+. After this I co-founded Depict.ai, scaled to serve billions of product recommendations and raised $20m from top investors.

Lovable is a small team of serial founders, product engineers, physicists, competitive programmers and people who care deeply about building a great product on be the first to make autonomous code generation work.… more

Post

A year ago, Lovable was fewer than 10 people. Today, we’re a team of 70.

One of the hardest parts of growing is holding on to what made us successful in the first place.

For us, that means speed and obsession about customers. That’s why we’re doubling down on individual ownership and cutting unnecessary meetings.

Meetings aren’t bad, alignment matters, but they should never be the default.

Ownership means trusting people to lead, make decisions, and stay accountable. It’s how we avoid bureaucracy and keep the startup spirit alive as we scale.

By the end of the year, we expect to be 100 exceptional people who all take on leadership and raise the bar.

2,597 · 123 comments

Post

Today Lovable raised $200M at a $1.8B valuation led by Accel. 

I've never shared how it started: I called my friend at 6AM to go for a walk...

I was CTO at Depict. We hit $1M ARR faster than any Swedish startup people heard of.
But after 3 years, something felt off. E-commerce optimization wasn't how I wanted to spend my life.

I kept thinking I want to do something better. 

In 2023, after ChatGPT 3 had just released, I started exploring what complex systems you could build with AI.

One weekend, I hacked together something simple: "plain english to working app."

I called it GPT Engineer. Posted it on GitHub. Went to sleep.

I woke up to chaos.

Over the next few weeks it got hundreds of thousands of users. 50k+ GitHub stars in weeks. My inbox was destroyed.

But the messages weren't from developers. They were from people who'd never coded before, building their first apps.
That's when it clicked.

The real insight: 99% of the world's best ideas are trapped in the heads of people who can't code.
They have problems. They know the solutions. They just can't build them.

AI shouldn't help engineers code faster. It should help everyone else build.

6AM. I called Fabian:
"Let’s go for a walk, I have an idea."
"When?"
"I’m outside."

He came down in slippers 10 minutes later. We walked through Stockholm for 2 hours.

By 8AM, we’d decided to start a company together.
Not to make coding easier.
To make it obsolete.

We're not trying to 'capture' the existing market for building apps and websites. We're creating a new market. 

Let me explain: 

Before Canva, there were only 5M 'designers' that used Photoshop. Canva made everyone capable of designing, 44x'd the market to 220M designers.

Lovable is doing the same for code and 'producing' 1 billion builders by building the product that builds all other products.

Today, there are 47M developers worldwide. Lovable is going to produce 1B potential builders. 

8 months in we're already seeing non-technical founders build successful software businesses with Lovable:

Caio Moretti -> made $3M in revenue in 48 hours with a product built on Lovable.

Airweave (agentic search tool for internal databases) -> recently secured admission to Y Combinator

Felipe -> hit $10k MRR with his laundry subscription product.

Building software takes 300+ hours. But when you can spin up a functional app in <1 hour, you can make builders out of everyone. 

Lovable already has 2.3M users building. 

Huge shoutout to the team, users, and investors.

We're not just democratizing code. We're helping everyone realize they have things to build.

empowering anyone to create

3d • 


Every change to any software system will go through ruthless AI-powered vulnerability analysis before hitting production. This isn't aspirational, it's where all of software is headed.

Today:
- Code changes are moving faster than manual review can scale
- Attack surfaces are expanding across dependencies, infrastructure, and integrations
- Security can't be a periodic audit or a last-mile gate if you want reliable outcomes

That's why we already run programmatic and AI-powered security tests on every change at Lovable.

The future looks like this:
- AI systematically analyzes attack surfaces at a scale and speed impossible to match manually
- Every pull request is automatically analyzed by simulating elaborate, real-world attack scenarios
- Analysis continues in gradual rollout and production, going down to performing real-life attacks and testing app defences in practice not theory

We're building the future of software security at Lovable. The goal is straightforward: make ruthless vulnerability analysis before production the standard operating mode for any team.

If you're a world-class security engineer who wants to help define what secure-by-default looks like in practice, we're hiring.… more

empowering anyone to create

1w • 


"Coding is no longer the bottleneck. Clarity is. Lazar spends 80% of his time planning and chatting with AI agents and only 20% building. The ceiling on AI output isn’t model intelligence; it’s what the model is told and shown before it acts."… more

View Lenny Rachitsky’s profile
Lenny Rachitsky Executive Top Voice Profile 2ndLenny Rachitsky  • 2nd

Deeply researched no-nonsense product, growth, and career advice

2w • 

My biggest takeaways from Lazar Jovanovic (a full-time vibe-coder at Lovable):

1. Vibe coding is now a full-time professional career. Lazar Jovanovic gets paid to build internal tools and public-facing products using AI. His work ranges from Shopify integrations and merch stores to complex internal dashboards tracking feature adoption. Companies across the S&P 500 are actively hiring people with these skills.

2. Not having a technical background can be an advantage when vibe coding. People without engineering experience don’t know what’s “supposedly impossible,” so they try things that technical people might dismiss. Lazar built Chrome extensions and desktop apps with Lovable because he didn’t know the technical reasons why those shouldn’t work. The willingness to try anything until proven wrong unlocks capabilities others overlook.

3. Coding is no longer the bottleneck—clarity is. Lazar spends 80% of his time planning and chatting with AI agents and only 20% building. The ceiling on AI output isn’t model intelligence; it’s what the model is told and shown before it acts. Your job is to provide clear context and instructions, not to write code.

4. AI tools have a limited context window that you must manage deliberately. Think of it like a genie granting you three wishes. Every request consumes tokens for reading, thinking, and executing. If you dump vague requests, the AI spends the most tokens figuring out what you want and has little left for quality output.

5. Start five parallel builds to find clarity your thinking faster. Instead of overthinking one design, brain dump into the first project, get more specific in the second, add visual references in the third, attach actual code snippets in the fourth, and compare all five. This costs more up front but saves hundreds of credits and days of iteration later.

6. When you get stuck, follow this four-step debugging sequence. First, let the tool try to fix it. Second, add console logs to track what’s happening and paste the output back to the agent. Third, bring in an external tool like Codex for deeper diagnosis. Fourth, revert a few steps and try a cleaner prompt.

7. Use rules files to make your findings permanent. After solving a problem, ask the AI how to prompt it better next time, and then add that guidance to your rules.md file.

8. Design and taste are becoming increasingly valuable in the AI era. When everyone can produce good-enough output instantly, the people who stand out are those who understand emotional design decisions—fonts, spacing, micro-interactions—that AI can’t yet replicate well. Invest time in studying elite design work.

9. Build in public to land vibe-coding jobs. Lazar got hired at Lovable because he was already shipping projects, teaching on YouTube, and sharing knowledge on LinkedIn. Hires at Lovable submitted Lovable apps instead of resumes. The fastest path to a professional vibe-coding role is doing the job before anyone pays you for it.… more

Lazar Jovanovic Premium Profile 2ndLazar Jovanovic  • 2nd

Professional Vibe Coding Engineer at Lovable | L5: Diamond Certified

Visit my website

3w • 

Stop trying to sound smart. 

When most people start building an app, their brain locks up because they think they need to sound technical, precise, and professional. 

But clarity doesn't come from structure. It comes from saying the thing out loud. 

For Day 2 of 28 Days of Lovable, we are throwing out the formal problem statement and replacing it with a story. 

If you can't explain your app to another human being, no AI is going to save you.

To move from idea to action, answer these three questions with zero jargon:

1. What problem am I actually trying to solve?
Don't use words like optimize or improve. Describe what is broken. If you are caring for a parent and feel lost juggling medications and portals, say exactly that. That is real and specific.

2. Who is this for?
Not users. Not people. Who is the specific person struggling with this right now? 

3. How should the solution feel?
This is the part most people skip, but it is your direction. Should it feel like a quiet mountain cabin or a high-speed cockpit? Use metaphors. Taste is your primary filter for design.

When you answer these, you shift from having an idea to understanding an experience. This becomes your first prompt. 

In Lovable, you can take this story into Plan Mode to generate an editable blueprint. You go from brain dump to execution without losing momentum.

You don't need a perfect framework to begin. You just need to be honest about what hurts and how relief should feel.

Catch Day 2 at https://lnkd.in/eWZShRpu… more

Sound familiar?

I work with successful content creators and coaches who are stuck in exactly this pattern.

The breakthrough isn't learning better prompts. It's getting a done-for-you system that runs your entire content pipeline automatically.

Here's what I build for you:

→ AI trained on YOUR voice, frameworks, and stories (no more generic robot outputs)
→ Automated pipeline from idea → draft → format → publish (no manual steps)
→ One-input, multi-platform distribution (create once, publish everywhere)
→ Content system that runs 24/7, even when you're not working

Post

We just raised another $25M after 10x'ing our ARR in 5 months. The crazy part is this almost never happened.

17 years ago, I watched Iron Man as a 10-year-old kid in Delhi. That night, I pulled my first all-nighter teaching myself to code. Not because I wanted to build apps or make money.

Because I wanted to build Jarvis.

My parents gave me 1 hour of screen time per day. So I coded in secret, sleeping every alternate night through middle school and high school. Built 50+ apps. Got a cease and desist from Google at age 12.

All for this one obsession: making computers understand us like humans do.

Fast forward to today:
- We've raised $81M total to build the voice operating system
- Growing revenue 40% month-over-month this year
- 70% user retention after one year (unheard of in consumer)
- Teams at 270 of the Fortune 500 use Wispr Flow daily

Our Series A2 was led by Hans Tung at Notable Capital (who was an early investor in five companies that made it to $100B valuation like Slack, Tiktok, and Airbnb). Steven Bartlett also joined as an investor and partner. 

But here's what matters more than the money:

We cracked voice input. Not transcription - actual understanding. Our users hit "send" in under 0.5 seconds without checking. They trust it blindly. That's never existed before.

In a recent benchmark, Wispr came out as 3-4x more accurate than OpenAI, ElevenLabs, and Siri. 

And we're just getting started. Voice input was step one. Now we're building the assistant that actually does things for you.

To my co-founder Sahaj Garg - there's no one else I'd rather build Jarvis with than my college roommate and closest friend.

To our team pulling all-nighters and shipping magic - you're the reason that 10-year-old kid's dream is becoming real.

We're hiring cracked engineers and growth marketers who want to build the future of human-computer interaction.

The keyboard had a good 150-year run.

Time to build what comes next.

PS: like, repost, and comment "Wispr" to get Wispr Flow for free for 3 months ❤️

3,132 · 549 comments

Post

Google sent me a cease and desist at 13 years old. I had 2.5M users for my previous app. And my parents had no idea.

I built it during the Limewire shutdown. You could type "play me the latest Imagine Dragons song" and it would find and download it instantly.

When it couldn't find a song online, it converted YouTube videos to MP3s.

Turns out, Google didn't like that.

One night at 3am, I got an email with words like "cease and desist," "juvenile," "illegal." I was terrified, couldn't sleep. But the worst part was still coming - telling my dad.

See, he thought I used my daily hour of screen time for Power Rangers. He had no idea I'd been coding from 10:30pm to 5am every night after they went to bed.

That Saturday morning, I handed my dad my laptop, with Google's legal threat on the screen, and watched his face as he discovered that his 13-year-old had built something used by millions.

Google killed the app. But that moment with my dad changed everything.

He didn't yell. Didn't punish me for keeping it secret. Instead, he asked: "How did you build this?"

As I explained the late nights and workarounds, I realized something: those constraints had shaped everything.

Limited screen time taught urgency.
Secret sessions killed perfectionism.
Fear of discovery meant ship now, iterate later.

Those constraints still drive how I work at Wispr Flow today. They forced me to focus on what mattered.

The world tells you to wait for better conditions.

But sometimes the magic happens precisely because conditions aren't perfect.

3,221 · 112 comments

Post

We'll pay top dollar for engineers who can solve this: How do you train AI to hear people that whisper to it?

Here's our challenge: Millions of users whisper to Wispr in open offices - speaking so quietly their coworkers can't hear them. They expect perfect transcription.

Traditional voice AI completely fails here. You can't create training data when expert labelers literally cannot decipher the audio. It's too quiet. Without context, it's just noise.

But users need this. They can't speak normally at their desks. So we have to invent entirely new ways to train AI.

This isn't even our hardest problem:

Infrastructure at breaking point: 1 billion words monthly, growing 10x in 6 months. Must maintain 700ms latency everywhere. Users literally dictate faster than we can scale servers.

Ambiguity beyond human capability: Whispered words without context are unsolvable. "Write," "right," "rite," or "Wright?" Even humans can't tell. We need AI that infers better than people.

Never repeat a mistake: Every user correction trains a personalized model. The bar: never make the same error twice for that user.

Individual writing DNA: Everyone has unique patterns - caps usage, punctuation preferences, formatting quirks. We're training LLMs for token-level personalization without losing accuracy.

We're not looking for people who work on solved problems.

We need engineers who see "impossible" as "not yet solved."

19 roles. Competitive comp for competitive problems.

The hardest technical challenges in voice AI are waiting.

610 · 142 comments

Post

Wispr Flow just raised a $30M Series A 🚀
(full investor list below)

Ever since the first Ironman movie in 2008, I wanted to build Jarvis. It inspired me enough that at age 10, I pulled my first all-nighter to teach myself how to code.

17 years later, we've found a way to make a voice experience delightful for all-day use. And there's no one else I'd build this with than my college roommate and closest friend, Sahaj Garg 🔥 

This year
⭐ We hit product-market twice
📈 Grew revenue by 50% each month
💻 Launched Flow across Mac, Windows, and iPhone
😍 Built the first voice interface that users trust blindly (users hit “Send” <0.5s on average)

Our Series A was led by Matt Kraning at Menlo Ventures (who previously sold Expanse for $1.25B). 

We were also fortunate to bring on some incredible angels including:
- Evan Sharp (Co-founder, Pinterest)
- Henry Ward (CEO, Carta)
- Flo Crivello (CEO, Lindy)
- Kenneth Schlenker (CEO, Opal)
- and more!

With this new funding, we’re building Flow into an intelligent assistant: so that in addition to writing what you say perfectly, it can take actions, help you proactively, and live across all your devices – becoming the single interface for the future of AI. 

What's next: We’re hiring cracked engineers and growth marketers who like charts that look like this 📈. 

Open roles are on our website. 

And finally, a huge shoutout to the entire Wispr Flow team who I've seen work tirelessly around the clock and grow both as professionals and people - without whom we wouldn't be on this incredible journey ❤️

Tanay Kothari Premium Profile 2ndTanay Kothari  • 2nd

CEO at Wispr Flow | IOI Medalist | Forbes 30 under 30 | Stanford CS + AI

6d • 


An 800-year-old brand taught me a growth strategy that enabled us to get millions of users. This is exactly how we implemented it: 

The brand is Chyawanprash - a health supplement.

Growing up in India, it was just always there. Every household had it. My parents used it, their parents used it.

There were no flashy ads to convinced them to buy it. 

It survived so long for one reason: someone they trusted told them it worked. And it did every single time.

I never thought much about how a health supplement earns 800 years of loyalty - until I started preparing for our India launch.

India doesn’t run on marketing. It runs on trust. And trust operates differently here than in most other markets.

People take more time before they trust something new. And once they do trust you, that loyalty runs deep.

We're seeing this firsthand with Wispr Flow.

In the US, most people start with monthly subscriptions. They want to try it out. See if it works. 

In India, about 75% of people choose annual plans right from the start.

That's not just about cost savings. It's about commitment.

When an Indian user decides to trust you and pay, they've already committed mentally. The sale is the confirmation of trust you've already built.

But here's what most founders miss: you can't rush that trust.

It's built through:

1/ Reliability: If your product breaks, people remember. But if it works consistently, they tell everyone they know. 

2/ Ownership: When something goes wrong, you don't hide. You own it. And you fix it publicly.

3/ Listening: Indian users will often tell you exactly what they need. When you actually act on it, they notice.

Chyawanprash survived 800 years by earning trust that families passed down through generations.

That's the standard we're building toward in India.

Because in India, when you earn someone's loyalty, you don't just get a customer - you get a champion who will support you for years.

That's the kind of foundation worth building.

— Written with Wispr Flow … more

Tanay Kothari Premium Profile 2ndTanay Kothari  • 2nd

CEO at Wispr Flow | IOI Medalist | Forbes 30 under 30 | Stanford CS + AI

1w • 


Steve Jobs' most famous quote has accidentally destroyed dozens of startups. 


People love quoting Steve Jobs: "Customers don't know what they want until you show it to them."

And they use it as permission to ignore feedback.

Big mistake.

Here's what Steve actually meant:

Customers won't tell you the solution. But they'll tell you the problem.

Bad founder behavior: → User says: "I want a flying car." → Founder says: "Okay, I'll build a flying car."

Good founder behavior: → User says: "I want a flying car." → Founder asks: "Why?" → User says: "Because I'm stuck in traffic for 2 hours every day and I can't get work done." → Founder thinks: "Oh. They don't want a flying car. They want to use their commute time productively."

Now you have options: 

- A shuttle service where they can work 
- Better remote work tools 
- A carpool that pairs people going the same direction

None of these are flying cars. But they all solve the real problem.

When we built Wispr Flow, users didn't say "I want AI-powered dictation that understands intent."

They said: "I find it tiring to respond to emails. I procrastinate on writing the blog I always wanted to. I'm overwhelmed by the number of messages."

So I looked into why.

Here's what I realized: when you go to write a message, you more or less know what you want to say.

But the moment you start actually typing, your brain switches gears. Now you're worrying about spelling, punctuation, how it sounds.

That's what was making it exhausting. Not the thinking - the translating.

So we built a tool that strips all of that away. You just speak your thoughts. 

If we'd just built what users asked for, we would've made a better voice note app. Instead, we solved the problem they actually had.

So yes - customers won't hand you the solution. But if you're not listening to their problems, you're just guessing.

And guessing kills companies.

— Written with Wispr Flow … more

Tanay Kothari Premium Profile 2ndTanay Kothari  • 2nd

CEO at Wispr Flow | IOI Medalist | Forbes 30 under 30 | Stanford CS + AI

1w • 


I stopped measuring my engineers by output. It’s led us to ship years of work in just 3 months.

Most engineering teams optimize for output: Tickets closed. Features shipped. Code written.

We optimize for something else: initiative.

Here's what I actually care about:

1/ Finding problems before they're assigned

The best engineers don't wait for a task. They notice something users are struggling with and propose a solution before anyone asks.

2/ Pulling people in at the right time

Collaboration doesn't mean consensus. It means knowing when to own something yourself and when to bring others in.

Bad engineers either work in silos or ask for permission on everything.

But great engineers know exactly when to do each.

3/ Impact, not input

Lines of code mean nothing. In fact, deleting code is usually better than writing it.

What actually matters: Did this move the product forward? Did users notice?

If I can't point to a measurable outcome, the work didn't matter.

The best weeks on our team aren't when we shipped what was planned. They're when someone fixed something that wasn't.

— Written with Wispr Flow … more

Tanay Kothari Premium Profile 2ndTanay Kothari  • 2nd

CEO at Wispr Flow | IOI Medalist | Forbes 30 under 30 | Stanford CS + AI

1w • Edited • 


The other day, I noticed something fascinating about Indian users: we hesitate before we type. That hesitation revealed a massive mistake dozens of companies are making.

As we prepared to launch Wispr Flow in India, I spent time with founders and operators there. That's when the pattern became clear.

People weren't hesitating because they didn't know what to say. But because there's a cost to communicating with technology that goes beyond language or price.

When I started asking why I found that the cost comes from three places:

1/ Cognitive cost: You think in one language. Structure it in another. Output it in a third.

That’s three mental translations before you hit send.

2/ Social cost: Language signals competence. And competence signals class.

One typo, one grammatical mistake - and people make assumptions.

So expression becomes performance.

3/ Technical cost: Software was built for people who think in English. But most people don't.

So you're forced to communicate in a way that doesn't match how you naturally think.

The keyboard wasn't removing these costs. It was spreading them out by forcing pauses, edits, backspacing.

India made this visible to us.

Everywhere people use technology, there's a gap between how we think and how we're forced to communicate.

But that gap disappears when you speak.

Voice removes all three costs at once. You think in one language, speak in that language, and Wispr Flow turns it into text in real-time.

We're not adapting to the workarounds people have built.

We're solving for what happens when natural expression no longer needs to be slowed down to feel safe.

That's what we're building for India.

— Written with Wispr Flow… more

Tanay Kothari Premium Profile 2ndTanay Kothari  • 2nd

CEO at Wispr Flow | IOI Medalist | Forbes 30 under 30 | Stanford CS + AI

1w • 


I spend 4 hours/day onboarding new employees. It’s one of the highest leverage tasks I do as a CEO. 

At 50 people, most founders delegate the onboarding process.

I do the opposite.

Every new hire spends their first two weeks in daily 1-on-1s with me.

Here's why:

Most companies fail to scale because new hires don't understand how the founder thinks.

You hire great people. But they make decisions differently than you would. So you get frustrated - all because you never taught them.

Here's what I do in those first two weeks:

1/ I ask them what they think our biggest priorities should be

Not what they should work on - what the company should focus on. Then I walk through my own reasoning.

Where we align, where we differ, and why I'm prioritizing what I'm prioritizing.

This isn't about being right. It's about showing them how I think.

2/ I watch how they work

I don't just review their output. I watch their process.

Where do they spend the most time? What do they skip?

Then I show them where I'd make different calls and why.

3/ I teach them how I think about making decisions

At Wispr, certain things matter more than others:

When is 80% good enough vs when do we need perfection?
When should you invest more time vs ship fast?
What defines "great" in design, in code, in customer conversations?

So I walk them through real examples, show them my reasoning, explain the tradeoffs.

Then they can make their own calls.

Sometimes they'll override me. And that's exactly what I want.

After two weeks, they're not just doing good work. They're doing work that moves us forward in the way we need.

— Written with Wispr Flow … more

Tanay Kothari Premium Profile 2ndTanay Kothari  • 2nd

CEO at Wispr Flow | IOI Medalist | Forbes 30 under 30 | Stanford CS + AI

We spent $0 on ads and just ran the most successful waitlist in voice AI history.

375,000 people signed up to wait for our Android app last week.

375,000 waitlist signups = 375,000 people who:
→ Read our landing page
→ Entered their email
→ Agreed to wait months
→ Many referred friends, shared on social and group chats

That's 28,125 hours of collective attention we earned, not bought.

Most companies spend millions to rent attention for seconds. These people gave us minutes, voluntarily.

But here's the truth:

375,000 waitlist signups sounds like a flex.

374,000 of them will probably churn if we ship something that's even 500ms too slow.

Voice isn't like other products. You don't get a second chance.

The bar isn't "good enough." The bar is "instant and perfect"—or you just go back to your keyboard.

Apple, Google, and Microsoft have spent billions on voice. Their output accuracy is 60%. Ours is 93%.

That gap is the entire reason half a million people are willing to wait.

But a waitlist is a loan on trust, not a deposit. We have to earn it back with every millisecond of latency, every dictation that lands perfectly on the first try.

Here's how we're preparing to pay it back:
→ Sub-500ms response time target
→ 6,000+ Android devices in our test matrix
→ 100+ languages supported from day one
→ No keyboard switching required

We didn't gamify this waitlist for vanity.

We gamified it because every referral, every shared link, every screenshot uploaded is someone telling their friend: "This is worth waiting for."

That's not a metric. That's a mandate.

Now we have to deliver.

What's the longest you've waited for a product, and was it worth it?… more

Tanay Kothari Premium Profile 2ndTanay Kothari  • 2nd

CEO at Wispr Flow | IOI Medalist | Forbes 30 under 30 | Stanford CS + AI

2w • 


A VC just did something I've never seen before. We had 6 years of runway and no plans to raise. Then she sent me a message that made us reopen our closed $30M round.

We'd just closed our Series A. We were burning $200K a month. 

We didn't need more capital.

Then I got a message from a VC at Notable Capital.

Most investor emails say: "Hey Tanay, I'd love to learn more about Wispr and your vision."

This one said: "I've tried every voice tool in the market. Wispr is my favorite. Here's why. I onboarded my entire family and my entire firm. I sat with each person through the experience. We have strong conviction."

I read it twice.

Wait. You did what?

She didn't ask for a demo. She became a power user. Then ran her own reference checks.

I took the call immediately.

Turns out, Notable Capital was formerly GGV. And GGV had Hans Tung - someone I'd met my first week at Stanford. I sat in on his talk and thought: "I want to work with this guy one day."

10 years later, here we were.

Over the next few weeks, we built conviction in each other. They saw the world the way we did.

We made an exception. Instead of waiting for Series B, we did an A2 with them.

Most investors ask founders to prove the product works. This investor proved it to herself first.

That level of effort is rare. When someone puts in that much work before they even talk to you, you pay attention. 

If you want a founder's attention, don't ask for a demo.

Do the work. Use the product. Form your own belief in it.

Then show up with proof, not questions.

That's what gets meetings. That's what closes rounds.

PS: Chelcie Taylor, you're one of the most special VC's I've worked with ❤️

— Written with Wispr Flow … more

Feb 2017 – Present

Publications
PENet—a scalable deep-learning model for automated diagnosis of pulmonary embolism using volumetric CT imaging

Nature Digital Medicine · Apr 24, 2020

Show publication
Pulmonary embolism (PE) is a life-threatening clinical problem and computed tomography pulmonary angiography (CTPA) is the gold standard for diagnosis. Prompt diagnosis and immediate treatment are critical to avoid high morbidity and mortality rates, yet PE remains among the diagnoses most frequently missed or delayed. In this study, we developed a deep learning model—PENet, to automatically detect PE on volumetric CTPA scans as an end-to-end solution for this purpose. The PENet is a 77-layer 3D convolutional neural network (CNN) pretrained on the Kinetics-600 dataset and fine-tuned on a retrospective CTPA dataset collected from a single academic institution. The PENet model performance was evaluated in detecting PE on data from two different institutions: one as a hold-out dataset from the same institution as the training data and a second collected from an external institution to evaluate model generalizability to an unrelated population dataset. PENet achieved an AUROC of 0.84 [0.82–0.87] on detecting PE on the hold out internal test set and 0.85 [0.81–0.88] on external dataset. PENet also outperformed current state-of-the-art 3D CNN models. The results represent successful application of an end-to-end 3D CNN model for the complex task of PE diagnosis without requiring computationally intensive and time consuming preprocessing and demonstrates sustained performance on data from an external institution. Our model could be applied as a triage tool to automatically identify clinically important PEs allowing for prioritization for diagnostic radiology interpretation and improved care pathways via more efficient diagnosis.… more

About
I help creators and brands master Generative AI, editing, and creator tools. With over 1M followers across Instagram, TikTok, and YouTube, I share practical tutorials, workflows, and strategies that make cutting-edge tech accessible to everyone.

As a Consultant, I work with brands to integrate AI into their creative processes, from content ideation to production. My work has led to partnerships with leading companies including Google, Samsung, Adobe, ASUS, Intel, Instagram, TikTok, and DJI.

Whether it’s teaching a creator how to edit more efficiently or helping a global brand develop an AI-powered content strategy, my mission is the same, to unlock creative potential with the tools shaping the future of content.… more

Featured
Post

I was invited to speak at the #1BillionSummit in Dubai about a hard truth most creators don’t want to hear:

AI isn’t the problem. Outdated prompting is.

In 2026, it’s no longer about which AI tool you use.
It’s about how you think, structure, and communicate intent to the model.

During my session, I broke down:

→Why “good looking” AI outputs are often fake good
→The hidden parameters creators ignore

→The common prompting traps that silently kill quality
→How Hollywood-level images and videos are actually engineered today

→Why the prompt is now a system, not a sentence
→The gap between average and exceptional AI content is no longer talent or tools.

It’s prompting literacy.
If your AI visuals and videos don’t look the way you want yet, don’t switch tools.

Upgrade the way you prompt.
That’s the real cheat code.

#1BillionSummit

3h • 

Who said AI images can’t realistically show emotions? 🚨

This time, we experimented with one of the hardest human emotions to replicate: Crying.

Not dramatic movie tears.
Real crying.

Behind every visual you see here is a structured prompt system.

Over time, we built layered prompt frameworks focused on specific human elements:

→ Realistic skin and micro-imperfections
→ Accurate racial and facial characteristics

→ Tear physics and moisture behavior
→ Subtle, believable facial tension

→ Natural body language and micro-expressions

Intentional prompting > random prompting.

Emotion isn’t generated.
It’s constructed.

We decided to share part of the system.

If you want access to the exact prompts and the method behind these images, comment PROMPT and I’ll send you the link.👇👇

1d • 


Here’s how to teleport anywhere with AI. 🚨
No green screen. No studio.

Just one photo and the right workflow.

This entire cinematic sequence was built in minutes using two tools and a simple scene-transition method that makes everything feel real.

Here’s exactly how we did it.

→ First: The Images.
We used Nano Banana Pro with image reference.

→ The key is not just prompting a background. It’s building a scene.
You can even reference specific locations or architectural styles to increase realism.

→ The more detailed your prompt, the more believable the composite.
Focus on accuracy in lighting, perspective, and human gestures so your subject actually feels placed inside the world, not pasted onto it.

→ Second: The Video.

→ We animated everything using Kling 3.0.
This is where most people break realism. The secret is continuity.

→ This creates seamless transitions instead of hard cuts.

→ Kling 3.0 can even generate sound effects during rendering, which dramatically reduces post-production time.

→ Final step: Editing.
→ We assembled everything in Premiere. Minimal cleanup. Mostly pacing, cuts, and light sound design polish.

Just strong scene building, accurate lighting logic, and motion continuity.
AI isn’t just generating images anymore.

It’s building believable presence.

1w • 


Photoshoots are dead. 🚨
At least the way we used to know them.

You no longer need a full crew, a rented studio, and a six-hour shoot day to launch a campaign that looks ultra realistic.

Today, you can recreate almost anything with AI and make it look like it came straight out of a Vogue editorial or a global brand campaign.

But there’s a catch.

It’s not about the tool.
It’s about the workflow.

If you don’t control the workflow, you don’t control consistency.
And without consistency, your “campaign” looks like random AI images stitched together.

So here’s our favorite workflow for insane character consistency and ultra-real results:

→ First: Gather your references

→ Collect your outfit inspiration (fashion direction), your location references (studio, rooftop, desert, Paris street, etc.), and define your model.

The clearer the inputs, the stronger the realism.

→ Next: Head over to Google Nano Banana

→ Use all your references to generate your main keyframe.
This is your hero shot.

Lock in the identity, outfit details, lighting style, and overall mood.
This frame defines everything.

→ Then: Prompt different angles Think like a real photographer. 
→ Low shot angle. → High angle. → Side profile. → Close-ups. → Detail shots 

You’re not generating random images. You’re directing a shoot.

→ If you need help crafting precise scene directions, ChatGPT can help you build structured, cinematic prompts that maintain character consistency.

→ After that: Move to Google Veo 3.1

→ Turn your strongest frames into video sequences.

→ Add subtle motion, camera movement, environmental realism.
Keep it grounded. Keep it cinematic.

→ Finally: Combine everything

→ Stitch visuals together and you have a full campaign in minutes.

If you want to master AI content creation and stay ahead of the curve, Join my private AI community → https://lnkd.in/dkyAmBZv

Follow Sebastien Jefferies 🧩 for more tech & AI insights.
… more

Featured
Post

KFC AI Food + Beverage Tests – Pt. II – “Ready 2 Roll”

This one has been simmering for a while, and I’m excited it finally gets to see the light of day.

The goal was to test the boundaries of current AI tools when it comes to photorealism in the food & beverage world - and to explore how well CGI and AI can merge for immersive storytelling.
Coming from a background in live-action tabletop food production, motion-controlled arms, high-speed cameras, and all the satisfying chaos that comes with live action traditional shoots, this was a fun one to dive into.

It all started analog - pen and paper sketches for the storyboard. Then came a rough lyrics sketch for a track, which I then refined with the help of an LLM and turned into a generated song using Suno - setting the tone and narrative for the storyboard. 

After some initial AI tests, I opted to quickly do the intro in 3D to allow for maximum control over the camera movements. 
This was followed by a deep dive into KFC’s campaign history to stay in line with their visual and tonal identity - as if this were a traditional agency-client setup. I wanted to see how far AI could go without relying on any new live-action footage.

From there, it was thousands of food images, cleanups, animation, VFX tweaks, and sound design to pull it all together.

Tools used: Runway, Pika, Kling AI, Google DeepMind Veo2, Luma AI and OpenAI Sora - each one brought something unique to the table, depending on the moment and technique.

And a big shoutout to Topaz Labs for inviting me to the Starlight early access program - genuinely impressed by what this new tool can do.

_______________________________

List of creatives who worked at the official KFC campaigns (as of my current knowledge):

@chuckstudios
@olafvangerwen_fooddirector
@jorisnoordenbos
@alehmadrid
@directedbyxander
@achowe
@director_ando
Scott Pickett

If you have worked on KFC campaign in past and would like to be listed here, please let me know.


Peace & love,
David

visual and stylistic qualities of traditional KFC campaigns using current AI technologies.

3,127 · 783 comments

Post

Fully AI-Generated Ad made in 4 weeks, featuring 14,847 images generated and approximately 1,250 videos created:

Divine Series - Chapter I: Venus (2025) 24" 
AI Spec Ad
#director David Blagojevic


I feel like we’re entering a new era of advertising and technology - one that could change the way we approach traditional commercial advertising. With our eyeballs indulged in beautiful and spectacular visuals every day, now seems like the perfect time for directors to reimagine ad formats and develop a fresh storytelling approach that helps viewers connect authentically with brands. 

Chapter I: Venus is the first in a series of 10 spec ads inspired by the divine Pantheon, blending beauty, luxury, and innovative storytelling formats with tabletop shots of the ingredients to round out the product experience.


For those passionate about the technical side, this project was the result of a four-week process involving the generation of 14,847 images and approximately 50 iterations per shot. With around 25 shots making up the final video, this translates roughly to 1,250 video generations in total. Huge thanks to Runway and their Creative Partners Program - this would not have been possible without you. 🙏

The process itself began traditionally: pen and paper in hand, sketching out the storyboard and defining the art direction. My goal was to craft a luxurious, minimalist, and avant-garde feel, deeply influenced by the sensual dark purple aura of the perfume. This was reflected in pastel purple jacaranda trees, rosy-purple sunsets, dramatic lighting, and the flowing gown of the lead character. Venus, as the embodiment of sensuality and femininity, became the cornerstone of the piece, shaping its overall aesthetic. Through the eyes of an everyday woman, the narrative aimed to feel ethereal and divine, as though inviting the viewer into a special Venusian temple - a place where the essence of the product could be truly experienced.

One of the more challenging aspects was emulating our style of live-action tabletop shots to showcase the perfume’s notes. I wanted a fresh yet traditional approach to these immersive sequences, tying them seamlessly to the narrative while presenting them in a unique way: through someone else’s lived experience of the product and its impact on their daily life. This approach borrows from the best of user-generated content on social media, evoking relatability while maintaining an elevated aesthetic - qualities that resonate so well on social media and build trust among users and clients.

As AI technology continues to advance, these processes are bound to become faster. However, designing a fresh perspective for the storyline and developing an art direction that aligns with the product’s essence will always require time and care.  


Let's connect - feel free to slide into my DMs to chat about anything AI-related! Peace and love 🙏

This is a non-commercial speculative ad and we do not have any affiliations with Chanel.


619 · 138 comments

Post

Sooo... This was a 29-day project with a whopping 13,212 images generated!

It took me considerably longer than I initially anticipated, but it was an incredible journey that helped me gain a solid grasp of the AI process from start to finish in terms of planning and execution.

I began with the classic approach of pen and paper, sketching out basic scenes in the form of a storyboard. Since this was an original idea with little to no references to start with, I dedicated time to research the brand, focusing on their visual style, mission, and the narrative they use to connect with their audience.

For the art direction, I explored inspirations from ancient Greece and Japan, eventually settling somewhere in between to create a unique look and feel.

My aim was to juxtapose the timeless elegance of ancient architecture with the modern luxury and ethereal beauty of women, their attire, and the product itself.

Then came the challenging part - bringing all of this to life on screen.

With 13,212 generated images, it involved extensive prompting, a fair amount of VFX clean-ups, matte painting, some 3D modeling, and more.

I’d love to hear your thoughts on this project!
Feel free to slide into my DMs if you’d like to chat about anything AI-related.

______________

AI visionaries pushing me to explore AI:

Curious Refuge – It all started here, and I’m still tuning in every week with new episodes, posts, or newsletter drops. You guys rock!
Dave Clark – His early work on spec ads laid the foundation for AI specs.
Paul Trillo – A true artist, timeless in any era. His work is of incredible value, no matter the time.
Alejandro Matamala-Ortiz and Cristóbal Valenzuela @ Runway – You changed everything for the better. Immeasurable thanks to you both.
Ryan Phillips – Your constant social activity keeps us all inspired and informed.
Benjamin Benichou – Your visuals and storytelling are absolutely out of this world.
Kavan The Kid – (IG: @kavanthekid) – This guy's talent and immersive storytelling make it easy to forget that you are watching AI content - just fully captivated.
Thank you all for doing your thing and inspiring us.

Peace and love 🙏

This is a speculative advertisement created using artificial intelligence.
We are not affiliated with Dior, and this video is entirely non-commercial, produced as a demonstration of AI's potential in advertising.
The individuals appearing in this video are not real; they have been generated by AI.

We strongly believe in protecting the rights of talents whose likeness may be used in advertising.
Our goal is to advocate for these individuals, ensuring they have the option to decide whether their image can be used, with proper compensation, or to decline entirely.
We are committed to finding a fair and ethical approach that respects their choices.

As we enter a new era of technology and marketing, let us be responsible in shaping the future.

David Blagojevic Verified Profile 3rd+David Blagojevic  • 3rd+

Commercial Film Director | AI Director |Runway + Pika Creative Partner |Beauty | Luxury | Cosmetics | Food + Beverage Tabletop

1yr • Edited • 


AI-Generated Food & Drink Tests: Part I

Coming from a background in food, drinks, and cosmetics tabletop production, I’ve spent years creating live-action scenes like this. Using Phantom cameras, custom-made rigs, camera robots, and precision triggers, we carefully synchronized every detail - from a robot arm’s camera movement to rigs launching, for example, steak into the air - and another rig creating a sauce cascade to hit it mid-flight.
Food styling and a not-so-small library of kitchen props were part of our everyday workflow. The process required incredible precision - prepping food, filming it while still fresh, and managing countless challenges along the way. It was always a collaborative effort that tested the entire team’s skill and patience.
When I first explored AI, I was curious to see how close we were to achieving photorealism with the tools available. Over the past months, I’ve experimented regularly, tracking how these tools are evolving and how they might streamline workflows without sacrificing quality.
This is one of my most recent tests, created with Kling, Runway, and Flux.

I’d love to hear your thoughts!… more

Commercial Film Director | AI Director |Runway + Pika Creative Partner |Beauty | Luxury | Cosmetics | Food + Beverage Tabletop

1yr • Edited • 


The Last Few Ears (2025) - Full AI Teaser (Director's Cut)
Director: David Blagojević

Sooo, this was a pretty challenging 3-weeks project - crafted for an exceptional client and their truly groundbreaking initiative.

This teaser introduces a series of Guinness World Record-breaking events led by the visionary Milos Maricic. The initiative’s profound mission is to unite people of all races, ethnicities, religions, and ages through something universal - the need to tell their story. Participants from around the globe will engage in one-on-one conversations with Miloš, sharing whatever they feel like sharing. These genuine moments of expression will then be transformed into Soulprint – an AI-generated digital artwork that captures the emotional spectrum of these conversations in a groundbreaking way.

From a creative perspective, this was an entirely AI-driven project. I began with traditional methods: crafting the storyboard and establishing a cohesive art direction to ensure the client’s vision stood out in its uniqueness, visuals, and emotional impact. By translating this vision into detailed cinematographic choices, I was able to achieve consistency in lighting, lenses, and composition.

To push the boundaries of AI technology, we developed and trained dozens of custom LoRas for this project. These specialized models allowed precise control over AI-generated visuals, capturing details such as unconventional human framing, the texture of goosebumps on skin, the nuance of a single tear, and other intricate emotional expressions.

A special thank you to Miloš and his team for entrusting me with this vision and allowing me the creative freedom to bring this concept to life. This project exemplifies the power of merging advanced AI technology with storytelling to create a deeply resonant experience.

Feel free to slide into my DM if you'd like to chat anything AI related.… more

About
1) Designing the future of AI at Lovable
2) Angel investing in early-stage startups
3) Sharing insights on design, AI, and startups

Obsessed with design and products since high school. Started my first company before I turned 18, studied business and design in the US, dropped out, became lead designer at Mapify (acquired by HomeToGo), and joined Gorillas as the first designer, helping it scale into a unicorn. Later joined Creandum as a design advisor, founded a product design & brand studio, became an angel investor in 15+ startups, and a podcast host.

Today, I lead Growth Design at Lovable. I split my time between Stockholm and Berlin, blending German functionality with Scandinavian minimalism in everything I do.

When I’m not working, you’ll find me surfing, skiing, snowboarding, running, or meeting new people.
Always on the move. Always connecting.

Introducing the Lovable x Shopify integration 🔥

Here's how I built a skincare store in under 10 minutes:

1/ Told Lovable to create a Shopify store for skincare.
→ It built the full store with layout, checkout, and cart ready to go.

2/ Added the products I wanted to sell.
→ Lovable uploaded them, generated product descriptions, and styled everything automatically.

3/ Said I was ready to launch.

Done. A working Shopify store, built entirely with prompts.
The new Shopify integration is now live for everyone.
